<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Wine</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the beverage. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Wine_(disambiguation)" title="Wine (disambiguation)">Wine (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a topical guide to this subject, see <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_wine" title="Outline of wine">Outline of wine</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3504" data-file-width="2336" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg/170px-Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg/255px-Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg/340px-Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wine_press_from_16th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century <a href="/wiki/Wine_press" title="Wine press">wine press</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Wine</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_drink" title="Alcoholic drink">alcoholic beverage</a> made from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermented</a> <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yeast_in_winemaking" title="Yeast in winemaking">Yeast</a> consumes the sugar in the grapes and converts it to <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, and heat. Different varieties of grapes and strains of yeasts produce different styles of wine. These variations result from the complex interactions between the biochemical development of the grape, the reactions involved in fermentation, the <a href="/wiki/Terroir" title="Terroir">terroir</a>, and the production process. Many countries enact legal <a href="/wiki/Appellation" title="Appellation">appellations</a> intended to define styles and qualities of wine. These typically restrict the geographical origin and permitted varieties of grapes, as well as other aspects of wine production. Wines not made from grapes include <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fruit_wine" title="Fruit wine">fruit wines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">cherry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">pomegranate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ribes" title="Ribes">currant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sambucus" title="Sambucus">elderberry</a>.
</p><p>Wine has been produced for thousands of years. The earliest known traces of wine are from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ6000</span> BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent8k_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent8k-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MichaelWong_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-MichaelWong-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ5000</span> BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indie_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-indie-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaeology96_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaeology96-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ4000</span> BC)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SicilyGuardian_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SicilyGuardian-8">[8]</a></sup>  although there is evidence of a similar alcoholic beverage being consumed earlier in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ7000</span> BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro-Sowinski_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-Sowinski-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hames_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hames-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGovern_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGovern-11">[11]</a></sup> The earliest  known winery is the 6,100-year-old <a href="/wiki/Areni-1_winery" title="Areni-1 winery">Areni-1 winery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oldest_Wine_Armenia_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oldest_Wine_Armenia-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOAUCLA_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IOAUCLA-13">[13]</a></sup> Wine reached the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> by 4500¬†BC and was consumed and celebrated in ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece_and_wine" title="Ancient Greece and wine">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrace#Thracians_and_wine" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome_and_wine" title="Ancient Rome and wine">Rome</a>. Throughout history, wine has been consumed for its <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxicating effects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_per_Drink_tables_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAC_per_Drink_tables-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_At_Specific_B.A.C._Levels_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_At_Specific_B.A.C._Levels-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wine has long played an important role in religion. <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">Red wine</a> was associated with <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptians" title="Ancient Egyptians">ancient Egyptians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and was used by both the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_Dionysus" title="Cult of Dionysus">cult of Dionysus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" title="Ancient Romans">Romans</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Bacchanalia" title="Bacchanalia">Bacchanalia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> also incorporates it in the <a href="/wiki/Kiddush" title="Kiddush">Kiddush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_wine" title="History of wine">History of wine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg/220px-Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg/330px-Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg/440px-Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Areni-1_cave_entrance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_wine" title="Armenian wine">Areni-1 cave</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> near the town of <a href="/wiki/Areni" title="Areni">Areni</a>. The cave is the location of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_wine" title="Armenian wine">world's oldest known winery</a> and where the <a href="/wiki/Areni-1_shoe" title="Areni-1 shoe">world's oldest known leather shoe</a> has been found.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest archaeological and archaeobotanical evidence for grape wine and viniculture, dating to 6000‚Äì5800 BC was found on the territory of modern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Both archaeological and genetic evidence suggest that the earliest production of wine elsewhere was relatively later, likely having taken place in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Southern Caucasus</a> (which encompasses <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, Georgia and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>), or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asian</a> region between <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Eastern Anatolia Region">Eastern Turkey</a>, and northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest evidence of a grape-based fermented drink was found in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ7000</span> BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro-Sowinski_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-Sowinski-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hames_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hames-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGovern_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGovern-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> from 6000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated128_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated128-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> from 5000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indie_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-indie-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> from 4000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SicilyGuardian_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-SicilyGuardian-8">[8]</a></sup> The earliest evidence of a wine production facility is the <a href="/wiki/Areni-1_winery" title="Areni-1 winery">Areni-1 winery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and is at least 6100 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oldest_Wine_Armenia_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oldest_Wine_Armenia-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOAUCLA_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-IOAUCLA-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Armenian_Trail_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Armenian_Trail-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hay_pers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="500" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hay_pers.jpg/170px-Hay_pers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hay_pers.jpg/255px-Hay_pers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hay_pers.jpg/340px-Hay_pers.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hay_pers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a relief of the eastern stairs of the <a href="/wiki/Apadana" title="Apadana">Apadana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, depicting <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> bringing an amphora, probably of wine, to the king.</div></div></div>
<p>A 2003 report by archaeologists indicates a possibility that grapes were mixed with <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> to produce mixed fermented beverages in China in the early years of the seventh millennium BC. Pottery jars from the Neolithic site of <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>, contained traces of <a href="/wiki/Tartaric_acid" title="Tartaric acid">tartaric acid</a> and other organic compounds commonly found in wine. However, other fruits indigenous to the region, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_hawthorn" title="Common hawthorn">hawthorn</a>, cannot be ruled out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> If these beverages, which seem to be the precursors of <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a>, included grapes rather than other fruits, they would have been any of the several dozen indigenous wild species in China, rather than <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a></i>, which was introduced there 6000 years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spread of wine culture westwards was most probably due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> who spread outward from a base of <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> coast of what are today <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Palestine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The wines of <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a> were exported to Egypt during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">Old Kingdom</a> and then throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>. Evidence includes two Phoenician shipwrecks from 750 BC discovered by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a>, whose cargo of wine was still intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> As the first great traders in wine (<i>cherem</i>), the Phoenicians seem to have protected it from oxidation with a layer of olive oil, followed by a seal of pinewood and resin, similar to <a href="/wiki/Retsina" title="Retsina">retsina</a>. Although the nuragic Sardinians already consumed wine before the arrival of the Phoenicians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="700" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg/220px-Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg/330px-Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg/440px-Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Georgian_%E2%80%9EKvevri%E2%80%9C.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Georgian <a href="/wiki/Kvevri" title="Kvevri">Kvevri</a> ancient wine vessel</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest remains of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Apadana Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> dating back to 515 BC include carvings depicting soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> subject nations bringing gifts to the Achaemenid king, among them <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> bringing their famous <a href="/wiki/Armenian_wine" title="Armenian wine">wine</a>.
</p><p>Literary references to wine are abundant in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> (8th century BC, but possibly relating earlier compositions), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkman" title="Alkman">Alkman</a> (7th century BC), and others. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, six of 36 wine <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphoras</a> were found in the tomb of King <a href="/wiki/Tutankhamun" title="Tutankhamun">Tutankhamun</a> bearing the name "Kha'y", a royal chief <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vintner" title="Vintner">vintner</a>. Five of these amphoras were designated as originating from the king's personal estate, with the sixth from the estate of the royal house of <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Traces of wine have also been found in central Asian <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> in modern-day China, dating from the second and first millennia BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="2400" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg/220px-Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg/330px-Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg/440px-Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Banquet_Louvre_Kylix_G133_by_Cage_Painter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wine boy at a <a href="/wiki/Symposium" title="Symposium">symposium</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:29-autunno,Taccuino_Sanitatis,_Casanatense_4182..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1062" data-file-width="1000" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg/220px-29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg/330px-29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg/440px-29-autunno%2CTaccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:29-autunno,Taccuino_Sanitatis,_Casanatense_4182..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressing_wine" title="Pressing wine">Pressing wine</a> after the harvest; <i>Tacuinum Sanitatis</i>, 14th century</div></div></div>
<p>The first known mention of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>-based wines in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is from the late 4th-century BC writings of <a href="/wiki/Chanakya" title="Chanakya">Chanakya</a>, the chief minister of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a>. In his writings, Chanakya condemns the use of alcohol while chronicling the emperor and his court's frequent indulgence of a style of wine known as <i>madhu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_pg_355-356_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_pg_355-356-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome_and_wine" title="Ancient Rome and wine">ancient Romans</a> planted vineyards near garrison towns so wine could be produced locally rather than shipped over long distances. Some of these areas are now world-renowned for wine production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The Romans discovered that burning sulfur candles inside empty wine vessels kept them fresh and free from a vinegar smell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> supported wine because the clergy required it for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eucharist_(Catholic_Church)" title="Eucharist (Catholic Church)">Mass</a>. <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">Monks</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> made wine for years, aging it in caves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_pg_62-63_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_pg_62-63-38">[38]</a></sup> An old English recipe that survived in various forms until the 19th century calls for refining white wine from bastard‚Äîbad or tainted <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bastardo_(grape)" title="Bastardo (grape)">bastardo</a></i> wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1486" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg/310px-Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg/465px-Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg/620px-Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Word_for_Wine_in_European_languages.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the word for wine in European languages.</div></div></div>
<p>The English word "wine" comes from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*winam</i>, an early borrowing from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>vinum</i>, "wine" or "(grape) <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vine</a>", itself derived from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> stem *<i>win-o-</i> (cf. <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>: <span lang="hy">’£’´’∂’´</span>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D5%A3%D5%AB%D5%B6%D5%AB" title="wikt:’£’´’∂’´">gini</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Œø·º∂ŒΩŒøœÇ</span> <i>oinos</i>; <a href="/wiki/Aeolic_Greek" title="Aeolic Greek">Aeolic Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">œù</a>Œø·øñŒΩŒøœÇ</span> <i>woinos</i>; <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a>: <i>wiyana</i>; <a href="/wiki/Lycian_language" title="Lycian language">Lycian</a>: <i>oino</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The earliest attested terms referring to wine are the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a> <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">êÄïêÄ∂êÄ∫êÑÄêÄöêÄ∫</span> <i>me-tu-wo ne-wo</i> (*<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ŒºŒ≠Œ∏œÖœùŒøœÇ ŒΩŒ≠œù·ø≥</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> meaning "in (the month)" or "(festival) of the new wine", and <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">êÄ∫êÄúêÄ∑êÄ¥êÄØ</span> <i>wo-no-wa-ti-si</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> meaning "wine garden", written in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Linear B also includes, inter alia, an <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideogram</a> for wine, i.e. <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">êÇñ</span>.
</p><p>Ultimate Indo-European origin of the word is the subject of continued debate. Some scholars have noted the similarities between the words for wine in <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a> (e.g. Armenian <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D5%A3%D5%AB%D5%B6%D5%AB" title="wikt:’£’´’∂’´">gini</a></i>, Latin <i>vinum</i>, Ancient Greek Œø·º∂ŒΩŒøœÇ, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> –≤–∏–Ω–æ <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[v ≤…™Ààno]</a></span>), <a href="/wiki/Kartvelian_languages" title="Kartvelian languages">Kartvelian</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%83%A6%E1%83%95%E1%83%98%E1%83%9C%E1%83%9D" title="wikt:·É¶·Éï·Éò·Éú·Éù">·É¶·Éï·Éò·Éú·Éù</a> <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Georgian" title="Help:IPA/Georgian">[…£vin…î]</a></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> (<i>*wayn</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> ◊ô◊ô◊ü <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[jaiin]</a></span>), pointing to the possibility of a common origin of the word denoting "wine" in these language families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Georgian word goes back to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Kartvelian" title="Proto-Kartvelian">Proto-Kartvelian</a> *<i>…£wino</i>-,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klimov_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-klimov-51">[51]</a></sup> which is either a borrowing from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klimov_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-klimov-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-s1-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-as1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-as1-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an1_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-an1-56">[56]</a></sup> or the lexeme was specifically borrowed from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Armenian" title="Proto-Armenian">Proto-Armenian</a> *<i>…£ ∑einyo</i>-, whence Armenian <i>gini</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klimov_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-klimov-51">[51]</a></sup> An alternate hypothesis by F√§hnrich supposes *<i>…£wino</i>- a native Kartvelian word derived from the verbal root *<i>…£un</i>- ('to bend').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> See <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Kartvelian/%C9%A3wino-" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Kartvelian/…£wino-">*<i>…£wino</i>-</a> for more. All these theories place the origin of the word in the same geographical location, Trans-Caucasia, that has been established based on archeological and biomolecular studies as the origin of viticulture.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_wine">Red wine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">Red wine</a></div>
<p>The red-wine production process involves extraction of color and flavor components from the grape skin. Red wine is made from dark-colored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grape_varieties" title="Grape varieties">grape varieties</a>. The actual <a href="/wiki/Wine_color" title="Wine color">color of the wine</a> can range from violet, typical of young wines, through red for mature wines, to brown for older red wines. The juice from most purple grapes is actually greenish-white; the red color comes from anthocyan pigments (also called <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>) present in the skin of the grape; exceptions are the relatively uncommon <a href="/wiki/Teinturier" title="Teinturier">teinturier</a> varieties, which actually have red flesh and produce red juice.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White_wine">White wine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/White_wine" title="White wine">White wine</a></div>
<p>Fermentation of the non-colored <a href="/wiki/Juice_vesicles" title="Juice vesicles">grape pulp</a> produces white wine. The grapes from which white wine is produced are typically green or yellow. Some varieties are well-known, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chardonnay" title="Chardonnay">Chardonnay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauvignon_blanc" title="Sauvignon blanc">Sauvignon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Riesling" title="Riesling">Riesling</a>. Other white wines are blended from multiple varieties; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokay_(grape)" title="Tokay (grape)">Tokay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sherry" title="Sherry">Sherry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sauternes_(wine)" title="Sauternes (wine)">Sauternes</a> are examples of these. Dark-skinned grapes may be used to produce white wine if the wine-maker is careful not to let the skin stain the <a href="/wiki/Wort" title="Wort">wort</a> during the separation of the pulp-juice. <a href="/wiki/Pinot_noir" title="Pinot noir">Pinot noir</a>, for example, is commonly used to produce <a href="/wiki/Champagne" title="Champagne">champagne</a>.
</p><p>Dry (non-sweet) white wine is the most common, derived from the complete fermentation of the <a href="/wiki/Wort" title="Wort">wort</a>. Sweet wines are produced when the fermentation is interrupted before all the grape sugars are converted into alcohol. Sparkling wines, which are mostly white wines, are produced by not allowing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the fermentation to escape during fermentation, which takes place in the bottle rather than in the barrel.
</p>
<h3><span id="Ros.C3.A9_wine"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ros√©_wine">Ros√© wine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ros%C3%A9_wine" title="Ros√© wine">Ros√© wine</a></div>
<p>A ros√© wine incorporates some of the <a href="/wiki/Wine_color" title="Wine color">color</a> from the grape skins, but not enough to qualify it as a <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">red wine</a>. It may be the oldest known type of wine, as it is the most straightforward to make with the <a href="/wiki/Maceration_(wine)" title="Maceration (wine)">skin contact method</a>. The pink color can range from a pale orange to a vivid near-purple, depending on the varietals used and wine-making techniques. There are three primary ways to produce ros√© wine: skin contact (allowing dark grape skins to stain the <a href="/wiki/Wort" title="Wort">wort</a>), saign√©e (removing juice from the <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a> early in fermentation and continuing fermentation of the juice separately), and <a href="/wiki/Blending_(alcohol_production)" title="Blending (alcohol production)">blending</a> (uncommon and discouraged in most wine growing regions). Ros√© wines can be made still, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-sparkling_wines" title="Semi-sparkling wines">semi-sparkling</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling</a>, with a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweetness_(wine)" title="Sweetness (wine)">sweetness levels</a> from <a href="/wiki/Dryness_(taste)" title="Dryness (taste)">dry</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proven%C3%A7al_(wine)" title="Proven√ßal (wine)">Proven√ßal</a> ros√© to sweet <a href="/wiki/White_Zinfandel" title="White Zinfandel">White Zinfandels</a> and blushes. Ros√© wines are made from a wide variety of grapes all over the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_encyclopedia_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_encyclopedia-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fruit_wines">Fruit wines</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fruit_wine" title="Fruit wine">Fruit wine</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fruit_wine" title="Fruit wine">Wines from other fruits</a>, such as apples and berries, are usually named after the fruit from which they are produced combined with the word "wine" (for example, <a href="/wiki/Apfelwein" title="Apfelwein">apple wine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elderberry_wine" title="Elderberry wine">elderberry wine</a>) and are generically called <a href="/wiki/Fruit_wine" title="Fruit wine">fruit wine</a> or country wine (not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> term <i><a href="/wiki/Vin_de_pays" title="Vin de pays">vin de pays</a></i>). Other than the grape <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a> traditionally used for wine-making, most fruits naturally lack either sufficient fermentable sugars, relatively low acidity, yeast nutrients needed to promote or maintain fermentation, or a combination of these three characteristics. This is probably one of the main reasons why wine derived from grapes has historically been more prevalent by far than other types, and why specific types of fruit wine have generally been confined to regions in which the fruits were native or introduced for other reasons.
</p>
<h3><span id="Mead_.28honey_wine.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mead_(honey_wine)">Mead (honey wine)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">Mead</a></div>
<p>Mead, also called honey wine, is created by fermenting <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> with water, sometimes with various fruits, spices, grains, or <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hops</a>. As long as the primary substance fermented is honey, the drink is considered mead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Mead was produced in ancient history throughout Europe, Africa and Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and was known in Europe before grape wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hornsey_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hornsey-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Starch-based_.22wine.22_and_wine-based_products"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Starch-based_"wine"_and_wine-based_products'>Starch-based "wine" and wine-based products</span></h3>
<p>Other beverages called "wine", such as <a href="/wiki/Barley_wine" title="Barley wine">barley wine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>), are made from starch-based materials and resemble <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>  more than traditional  wine, while <a href="/wiki/Ginger_wine" title="Ginger wine">ginger wine</a> is fortified with <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a>. In these latter cases, the term "wine" refers to the similarity in alcohol content rather than to the production process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The commercial use of the English word "wine" (and its equivalent in other languages) is protected by law in many jurisdictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wine_Label_decoder_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wine_Label_decoder-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some UK supermarkets have been criticised for selling ‚Äúwine based‚Äù drinks, which only contain 75% wine, but which are still marketed as wine. The International Organisation of Vine and Wine requires that a "wine based drink" must contain a minimum of 75% wine, but producers do not have to divulge the nature of the remaining 25%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grape_varieties">Grape varieties</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grape_Vineyard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Grape_Vineyard.jpg/220px-Grape_Vineyard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Grape_Vineyard.jpg/330px-Grape_Vineyard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Grape_Vineyard.jpg/440px-Grape_Vineyard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grape_Vineyard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grape vineyard</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_grape_varieties" title="List of grape varieties">List of grape varieties</a></div>
<p>Wine is usually made from one or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variety_(biology)" title="Variety (biology)">varieties</a> of the European <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a></i>, such as <a href="/wiki/Pinot_noir" title="Pinot noir">Pinot noir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chardonnay" title="Chardonnay">Chardonnay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_Sauvignon" title="Cabernet Sauvignon">Cabernet Sauvignon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamay" title="Gamay">Gamay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlot" title="Merlot">Merlot</a>. When one of these varieties is used as the predominant grape (usually defined by law as minimums of 75% to 85%), the result is a "<a href="/wiki/Varietal" title="Varietal">varietal</a>" as opposed to a "blended" wine. Blended wines are not necessarily inferior to varietal wines, rather they are a different style of wine-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wine can also be made from other species of grape or from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_(grapes)" title="Hybrid (grapes)">hybrids</a>, created by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_crossing_(vine)" title="Genetic crossing (vine)">genetic crossing</a> of two species. <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_labrusca" title="Vitis labrusca">V. labrusca</a></i> (of which the <a href="/wiki/Concord_grape" title="Concord grape">Concord grape</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_aestivalis" title="Vitis aestivalis">V. aestivalis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_rupestris" title="Vitis rupestris">V. rupestris</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_rotundifolia" title="Vitis rotundifolia">V. rotundifolia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_riparia" title="Vitis riparia">V. riparia</a></i> are native <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> grapes usually grown to eat fresh or for grape juice, jam, or jelly, and only occasionally made into wine.
</p><p>Hybridization is different from <a href="/wiki/Grafting" title="Grafting">grafting</a>. Most of the world's vineyards are planted with European <i>V. vinifera</i> vines that have been grafted onto North American species' rootstock, a common practice due to their resistance to <a href="/wiki/Phylloxera" title="Phylloxera">phylloxera</a>, a root louse that eventually kills the vine. In the late 19th century, most of Europe's vineyards (excluding some of the driest in the south) were devastated by the infestation, leading to widespread vine deaths and eventual replanting. Grafting is done in every wine-producing region in the world except in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>‚Äîthe only places not yet exposed to the insect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_pg_97_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson_pg_97-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the context of wine production, <i><a href="/wiki/Terroir" title="Terroir">terroir</a></i> is a concept that encompasses the varieties of grapes used, elevation and shape of the vineyard, type and chemistry of soil, climate and seasonal conditions, and the local yeast cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The range of possible combinations of these factors can result in great differences among wines, influencing the fermentation, finishing, and aging processes as well. Many wineries use growing and production methods that preserve or accentuate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aroma_(wine)" title="Aroma (wine)">aroma</a> and taste influences of their unique <i>terroir</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_22-23_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_22-23-73">[73]</a></sup> However, flavor differences are less desirable for producers of mass-market <a href="/wiki/Table_wine" title="Table wine">table wine</a> or other cheaper wines, where consistency takes precedence. Such producers try to minimize differences in sources of grapes through production techniques such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro-oxygenation" title="Micro-oxygenation">micro-oxygenation</a>, tannin filtration, cross-flow filtration, thin-film evaporation,
and spinning cones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_wine" title="Classification of wine">Classification of wine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wine_grapes03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1060" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/170px-Wine_grapes03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/255px-Wine_grapes03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/340px-Wine_grapes03.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wine_grapes03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wine grapes on a vine</div></div></div>
<p>Regulations govern the classification and sale of wine in many regions of the world. European wines tend to be classified by region (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_wine" title="Bordeaux wine">Bordeaux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rioja_(wine)" title="Rioja (wine)">Rioja</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chianti" title="Chianti">Chianti</a>), while non-European wines are most often classified by grape (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Pinot_noir" title="Pinot noir">Pinot noir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlot" title="Merlot">Merlot</a>). Market recognition of particular regions has recently been leading to their increased prominence on non-European wine labels. Examples of recognized non-European locales include <a href="/wiki/Napa_Valley_AVA" title="Napa Valley AVA">Napa Valley</a>, Santa Clara Valley, <a href="/wiki/Sonoma_Valley_AVA" title="Sonoma Valley AVA">Sonoma Valley</a>, Anderson Valley, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendocino_County" title="Mendocino County">Mendocino County</a> in California; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willamette_Valley_(wine)" title="Willamette Valley (wine)">Willamette Valley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rogue_Valley_AVA" title="Rogue Valley AVA">Rogue Valley</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oregon_(wine)" title="Oregon (wine)">Oregon</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_Valley_(wine)" title="Columbia Valley (wine)">Columbia Valley</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_(wine)" title="Washington (wine)">Washington</a>; <a href="/wiki/Barossa_Valley_(wine)" title="Barossa Valley (wine)">Barossa Valley</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Australia_(wine)" title="South Australia (wine)">South Australia</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter_Valley_(wine)" title="Hunter Valley (wine)">Hunter Valley</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_(wine)" title="New South Wales (wine)">New South Wales</a>; Luj√°n de Cuyo in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argentina_(wine)" title="Argentina (wine)">Argentina</a>; Central Valley in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chile_(wine)" title="Chile (wine)">Chile</a>; Vale dos Vinhedos in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>; <a href="/wiki/Hawke%27s_Bay_Region" title="Hawke's Bay Region">Hawke's Bay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_Region" title="Marlborough Region">Marlborough</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_(wine)" title="New Zealand (wine)">New Zealand</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Canadian_wine" title="Canadian wine">Canada</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okanagan_Valley_(wine)" title="Okanagan Valley (wine)">Okanagan Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia_wine" title="British Columbia wine">British Columbia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Peninsula" title="Niagara Peninsula">Niagara Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Essex_County,_Ontario" title="Essex County, Ontario">Essex County</a> regions of <a href="/wiki/Ontario_wine" title="Ontario wine">Ontario</a> are the three largest producers.
</p><p>Some blended wine names are marketing terms whose use is governed by <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> law rather than by specific wine laws. For example, <a href="/wiki/Meritage" title="Meritage">Meritage</a> (sounds like "heritage") is generally a Bordeaux-style blend of <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_Sauvignon" title="Cabernet Sauvignon">Cabernet Sauvignon</a> and Merlot, but may also include <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_Franc" title="Cabernet Franc">Cabernet Franc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petit_Verdot" title="Petit Verdot">Petit Verdot</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malbec" title="Malbec">Malbec</a>. Commercial use of the term Meritage is allowed only via licensing agreements with the Meritage Association.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_classifications">European classifications</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1224" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg/170px-Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg/255px-Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg/340px-Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscato_d%27Asti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Moscato_d%27Asti" title="Moscato d'Asti">Moscato d'Asti</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Denominazione_di_origine_controllata" title="Denominazione di origine controllata">DOCG</a> wine</div></div></div>
<p>France has various <a href="/wiki/Appellation" title="Appellation">appellation</a> systems based on the concept of <i>terroir</i>, with classifications ranging from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vin_de_Table" title="Vin de Table">Vin de Table</a></i> ("table wine") at the bottom, through <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vin_de_Pays" title="Vin de Pays">Vin de Pays</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Appellation_d%27Origine_Vin_D%C3%A9limit%C3%A9_de_Qualit%C3%A9_Sup%C3%A9rieure" title="Appellation d'Origine Vin D√©limit√© de Qualit√© Sup√©rieure">Appellation d'Origine Vin D√©limit√© de Qualit√© Sup√©rieure</a></i> (AOVDQS), up to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Appellation_d%27Origine_Contr%C3%B4l%C3%A9e" title="Appellation d'Origine Contr√¥l√©e">Appellation d'Origine Contr√¥l√©e</a></i> (AOC) or similar, depending on the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> has developed a system resembling that of France and, in fact, pioneered this concept in 1756 with a royal charter creating the Demarcated Douro Region and regulating the production and trade of wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Germany created a similar scheme in 2002, although it has not yet achieved the authority of the other countries' classification systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> have classifications based on a dual system of region of origin and product quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_Europe">Beyond Europe</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_World_wines" title="New World wines">New World wines</a>‚Äîthose made outside the traditional wine regions of Europe‚Äîare usually classified by grape rather than by <i>terroir</i> or region of origin, although there have been unofficial attempts to classify them by quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Canadian Food and Drug Regulations, wine in Canada is an alcoholic beverage that is produced by the complete or partial alcoholic fermentation of fresh grapes, grape must, products derived solely from fresh grapes, or any combination of them. There are many materials added during the course of the manufacture, such as yeast, concentrated grape juice, dextrose, fructose, glucose or glucose solids, invert sugar, sugar, or aqueous solutions. Calcium sulphate in such quantity that the content of soluble sulphates in the finished wine shall not exceed 0.2 per cent weight by volume calculated as potassium sulphate. Calcium carbonate in such quantity that the content of tartaric acid in the finished wine shall not be less than 0.15 per cent weight by volume. Also, sulphurous acid, including salts thereof, in such quantity that its content in the finished wine shall not exceed 70 parts per million in the free state, or 350 parts per million in the combined state, calculated as sulphur dioxide. Caramel, amylase and pectinase at a maximum level of use consistent with good manufacturing practice. Brandy, fruit spirit or alcohol derived from the alcoholic fermentation of a food source distilled to not less than 94 per cent alcohol by volume. Prior to final filtration may be treated with a strongly acid cation exchange resin in the sodium ion form, or a weakly basic anion exchange resin in the hydroxyl ion form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vintages">Vintages</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vintage" title="Vintage">Vintage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG/200px-Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG/300px-Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG/400px-Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Champagne_mill%C3%A9sim%C3%A9_1995.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vintage French Champagne</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, for a wine to be vintage-dated and labeled with a country of origin or <a href="/wiki/American_Viticultural_Area" title="American Viticultural Area">American Viticultural Area</a> (AVA; e.g., <a href="/wiki/Sonoma_Valley" title="Sonoma Valley">Sonoma Valley</a>), 95% of its volume must be from grapes harvested in that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27_CFR_4.27_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-27_CFR_4.27-84">[84]</a></sup> If a wine is not labeled with a country of origin or AVA the percentage requirement is lowered to 85%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27_CFR_4.27_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-27_CFR_4.27-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vintage wines are generally bottled in a single batch so that each bottle will have a similar taste. Climate's impact on the character of a wine can be significant enough to cause different vintages from the same vineyard to vary dramatically in flavor and quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Thus, vintage wines are produced to be individually characteristic of the particular vintage and to serve as the flagship wines of the producer. Superior vintages from reputable producers and regions will often command much higher prices than their average ones. Some vintage wines (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Brunello_di_Montalcino" title="Brunello di Montalcino">Brunello</a>), are only made in better-than-average years.
</p><p>For consistency, non-vintage wines can be blended from more than one vintage, which helps wine-makers sustain a reliable market image and maintain sales even in bad years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> One recent study suggests that for the average wine drinker, the vintage year may not be as significant for perceived quality as had been thought, although wine connoisseurs continue to place great importance on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tasting">Tasting</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tempranillowine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="980" data-file-width="1364" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tempranillowine.jpg/220px-Tempranillowine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tempranillowine.jpg/330px-Tempranillowine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tempranillowine.jpg/440px-Tempranillowine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tempranillowine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Judging <a href="/wiki/Wine_color" title="Wine color">color</a> is the first step in tasting a wine.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wine_tasting" title="Wine tasting">Wine tasting</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wine_tasting_descriptors" title="Wine tasting descriptors">Wine tasting descriptors</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wine_tasting" title="Wine tasting">Wine tasting</a> is the sensory examination and evaluation of wine. Wines contain many chemical compounds similar or identical to those in fruits, vegetables, and <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sweetness_of_wine" title="Sweetness of wine">sweetness of wine</a> is determined by the amount of residual sugar in the wine after fermentation, relative to the acidity present in the wine. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dry_wine" title="Dry wine">Dry wine</a>, for example, has only a small amount of residual sugar. Some wine labels suggest opening the bottle and letting the wine "breathe" for a couple of hours before serving, while others recommend drinking it immediately. <a href="/wiki/Decanter" title="Decanter">Decanting</a> (the act of pouring a wine into a special container just for breathing) is a controversial subject among wine enthusiasts. In addition to aeration, decanting with a filter allows the removal of bitter sediments that may have formed in the wine. Sediment is more common in older bottles, but aeration may benefit younger wines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_46_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_46-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>During aeration, a younger wine's exposure to air often "relaxes" the drink, making it smoother and better integrated in aroma, texture, and flavor. Older wines generally fade (lose their character and flavor intensity) with extended aeration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Despite these general rules, breathing does not necessarily benefit all wines. Wine may be tasted as soon as the bottle is opened to determine how long it should be aerated, if at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (April 2018)">better¬†source¬†needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When tasting wine, individual flavors may also be detected, due to the complex mix of organic molecules (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>) that grape juice and wine can contain. Experienced tasters can distinguish between flavors characteristic of a specific grape and flavors that result from other factors in wine-making. Typical intentional flavor elements in wine‚Äîchocolate, vanilla, or coffee‚Äîare those imparted by aging in oak casks rather than the grape itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizontal_tasting" title="Horizontal tasting">Vertical and horizontal tasting</a> involves a range of vintages within the same grape and vineyard, or the latter in which there is one vintage from multiple vineyards. "<a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">Banana</a>" flavors (<a href="/wiki/Isoamyl_acetate" title="Isoamyl acetate">isoamyl acetate</a>) are the product of yeast metabolism, as are spoilage aromas such as "medicinal" or "Band-Aid" (<a href="/wiki/4-Ethylphenol" title="4-Ethylphenol">4-ethylphenol</a>), "spicy" or "smoky" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4-ethylguaiacol" title="4-ethylguaiacol">4-ethylguaiacol</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and rotten egg (<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Some varieties can also exhibit a mineral flavor due to the presence of water-soluble salts as a result of limestone's presence in the vineyard's soil. Wine aroma comes from volatile compounds released into the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Vaporization of these compounds can be accelerated by twirling the wine glass or serving at room temperature. Many drinkers prefer to chill red wines that are already highly aromatic, like <a href="/wiki/Chinon_AOC" title="Chinon AOC">Chinon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beaujolais" title="Beaujolais">Beaujolais</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_44-45_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_&amp;_Robinson_pg_44-45-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ideal temperature for serving a particular wine is a matter of debate by wine enthusiasts and sommeliers, but some broad guidelines have emerged that will generally enhance the experience of tasting certain common wines. A white wine should foster a sense of coolness, achieved by serving at "cellar temperature" (13¬†¬∞C (55¬†¬∞F)). Light red wines drunk young should also be brought to the table at this temperature, where they will quickly rise a few degrees.  Red wines are generally perceived best when served <i>chambr√©</i> ("at room temperature"). However, this does not mean the temperature of the dining room‚Äîoften around 21¬†¬∞C (70¬†¬∞F)‚Äîbut rather the coolest room in the house and, therefore, always slightly cooler than the dining room itself.  Pinot noir should be brought to the table for serving at 16¬†¬∞C (61¬†¬∞F) and will reach its full bouquet at 18¬†¬∞C (64¬†¬∞F). Cabernet Sauvignon, zinfandel, and Rhone varieties should be served at 18¬†¬∞C (64¬†¬∞F) and allowed to warm on the table to 21¬†¬∞C (70¬†¬∞F) for best aroma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collecting">Collecting</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_wine" title="Aging of wine">Aging of wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Investment_wine" title="Investment wine">Investment wine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Storage_of_wine" title="Storage of wine">Storage of wine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaux94_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1220" data-file-width="650" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Margaux94_1.jpg/170px-Margaux94_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Margaux94_1.jpg/255px-Margaux94_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Margaux94_1.jpg/340px-Margaux94_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaux94_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_Margaux" title="Ch√¢teau Margaux">Ch√¢teau Margaux</a>, a <a href="/wiki/First_Growth" title="First Growth">First Growth</a> from the Bordeaux region of France, is highly collectible.</div></div></div>
<p>Outstanding vintages from the best vineyards may sell for thousands of <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">dollars</a> per bottle, though the broader term "fine wine" covers those typically retailing in excess of US$30‚Äì50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Investment_wine" title="Investment wine">Investment wines</a>" are considered by some to be <a href="/wiki/Veblen_good" title="Veblen good">Veblen goods</a>: those for which demand increases rather than decreases as their prices rise.
Particular selections have higher value, such as "Verticals", in which a range of vintages of a specific grape and vineyard, are offered. The most notable was a <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27Yquem#Since_1968" title="Ch√¢teau d'Yquem">Ch√¢teau d'Yquem</a> 135 year vertical containing every vintage from 1860 to 2003 sold for $1.5 million.
The most common wines purchased for investment include those from <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_wine" title="Bordeaux wine">Bordeaux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_wine" title="Burgundy wine">Burgundy</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cult_wine" title="Cult wine">cult wines</a> from <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and elsewhere; and <a href="/wiki/Port_wine" title="Port wine">vintage port</a>. Characteristics of highly collectible wines include:
</p>
<ol><li>A proven track record of holding well over time</li>
<li>A drinking-window plateau (i.e., the period for maturity and approachability) that is many years long</li>
<li>A consensus among experts as to the quality of the wines</li>
<li>Rigorous production methods at every stage, including grape selection and appropriate barrel aging</li></ol>
<p>Investment in fine wine has attracted those who take advantage of their victims' relative ignorance of this wine market sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Wine_fraud" title="Wine fraud">wine fraudsters</a> often profit by charging excessively high prices for off-vintage or lower-status wines from well-known wine regions, while claiming that they are offering a sound investment unaffected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_cycle" title="Economic cycle">economic cycles</a>. As with any investment, thorough research is essential to making an informed decision.
</p>

<p><small>* May include official, semi-official or estimated data.</small>
</p><p>Wine grapes grow almost exclusively between 30 and 50 degrees latitude north and south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. The world's southernmost vineyards are in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Otago" title="Central Otago">Central Otago</a> region of New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a> near the <a href="/wiki/45th_parallel_south" title="45th parallel south">45th parallel south</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and the northernmost are in <a href="/wiki/Flen" title="Flen">Flen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, just north of the <a href="/wiki/59th_parallel_north" title="59th parallel north">59th parallel north</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exporting_countries">Exporting countries</span></h3>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:80%;"><tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<table class="wikitable">

<p>The UK was the world's largest importer of wine in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span></h2>
<p>Wine-consumption data from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_alcohol_consumption" title="List of countries by alcohol consumption">list of countries by alcohol consumption</a> measured in liters of pure ethyl alcohol consumed <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> in a given year, according to the most recent data from the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>. The methodology includes persons 15 years of age or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
